Jamison Brereton

86 (912)
Vr╠еs╠гa╠Дkapi (Conversation of Vr╠еs╠гa╠Дkapi Aindra, Indra╠Дn╠г─▒, ╠Д and Indra)
Indra (1, 8, 11тАУ12, 14, 19тАУ22), Indr─Бс╣З─л (2тАУ6, 9тАУ10, 15тАУ18), Vrс╣г─Б╠е kapi Aindra (7, 13, 23) [per the Anukramaс╣З─л]
23 verses: paс╣Еkti

This dialogue hymn was called by Renou тАЬthe strangest poem in the R╠еgvedaтАЭ (тАЬle po├иme le plus ├йtrange du RV,тАЭ 1956: 246). The three participants in the dialogue are Indra, his wife Indr─Бс╣З─л, and his raffish monkey pal Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, who is making sexual advances to Indr─Бс╣З─л. The hymn contains startlingly explicit sexual discourse, as well as a dazzling array of apparent non-sequiturs, all embedded in an exaltation of Indra, signaled by its consistent refrain.

Not surprisingly its meaning and web of references have been the subject of much controversy; we cannot discuss the many conflicting interpretations here. However, in our opinion many of the puzzles can be solved if it is interpreted as an oblique treatment of the A┼Ыvamedha (Horse Sacrifice) ritual, a royal ritual in which, according to the middle Vedic ritual texts, the chief queen copulates with the sacrificed horse to increase and magnify the power of the king, her husband. In this reading, the monkey represents the horse, with whom Indr─Бс╣З─л must have sexual relations. She both indignantly rejects and also encourages Vr╠ес╣г─БkapiтАЩs advances in the course of the hymn, and after their ritual copulation she is called тАЬwife of Vr╠ес╣г─БkapiтАЭ (vs. 13). The power of her husband Indra, the king, is extended by the performance of this ritual, though he has been, in effect, cuckolded. Hence the paradoxical announcement in verse 17, which proclaims the sexual тАЬloserтАЭ the real master. For a detailed discussion of the hymn in the context of the A┼Ыvamedha, see Jamison (1996a: 74тАУ88).

The hymn begins with IndraтАЩs complaint that he is no longer receiving honor or soma, a statement echoed by his wife (vss. 1тАУ2). The falling off of ritual obser vance sets the stage for the performance of an A┼Ыvamedha, for Indra to regain his position. The topic then shifts to Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi (vss. 3тАУ6), whose friendship with Indra is clearly a bone of contention between the married couple. Indr─Бс╣З─л reveals in annoyance that he has made advances to her, but then, in a startling descent into vulgar speech, boasts of her own sexual prowess (vs. 6). Vulgarity is matched by vulgarity in Vr╠ес╣г─БkapiтАЩs first entry into the conversation (vs. 7), where he responds to her implicit invitation. These two verses may well verbally signal the sexual activity between them, the climactic moment of interspecies pairing that mimics the A┼Ыvamedha. The next two verses (8тАУ9), presumably spoken by Indra and Indr─Бс╣З─л, may well be a humorous reflection of this intercourse, which may appear to Indra as spectator more like vexation than pleasure.

In our view, the next two verses (10тАУ11) are spoken by the narrator, proclaiming Indr─Бс╣З─лтАЩs important new ritual status, as the queen ensuring the life and prosper ity of her husband Indra. (The Anukramaс╣З─л assigns them to Indra.) Indra then laments the loss of his friend (vs. 12), who is going to the gods as sacrificial offer ing, but rejoices in the renewed sacrificial offerings he himself is receiving (vss. 13тАУ14). In these verses he addresses his wife both as Indr─Бс╣З─л (тАЬwife of Indra,тАЭ vs. 12) and Vr╠ес╣г─Бkap─Бy─л (тАЬwife of Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi,тАЭ vs. 13), for by her copulation with the monkey she became his temporary ritual wife. The narrator affirms the renewal of soma offering to Indra in verse 15, rectifying the lapse in ritual attentions noted in verse 1.

The two responsive verses that follow (16тАУ17) are the heart of the hymn and the best support for and justification of the A┼Ыvamedha. The first contains what would be a universally accepted truism: the sexually successful male is the master and has the power. But this is trumped by the paradoxical reversal: the sexually successful male does not have the power; itтАЩs the unsuccessful one who is master. This state
ment is beautifully applicable to the A┼Ыvamedha: the king is cuckolded by a horse (or, in this case, a monkey) and is witness to this humiliating act, but the king also receives all the benefit from the ritual, which increases his power, while the sexual partner of the queen is sacrificed.

With this pair of verses the hymn has reached its high point and conveyed its message. The remaining verses are something of a letdown. The next five (vss. 18тАУ22) are an extended pun, relating the yearlong journey of the horse/monkey before the sacrifice with its journey to the gods after its sacrifice. The final puzzling verse (23) superficially has nothing to do with the rest of the hymn, but relates a miraculous birth from the daughter of Manu, who has the speaking name тАЬRib.тАЭ The deeper connection must be the explosive fertility that the performance of the A┼Ыvamedha should confer on its participants. For discussion of this verse in an Indo-European context, see Watkins (2004).

The A┼Ыvamedha is treated more directly in two hymns in Maс╣Зс╕Нala I, I.162 and 163.

рдорд╛рдирд╕рддрд░рдЩреНрдЧрд┐рдгреАрдХреГрддреН - рдЯрд┐рдкреНрдкрдиреА

This Erikepaius is a likely ortholog of none other than our Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi, who is celebrated in RV 10.86. He is described as a friend of Indra and was later identified with Viс╣гс╣Зu (we do not know if that identification already existed in the RV). The great patriot Tilak identified him with the Orion region of the sky that is more commonly associated with the Hindu protogonic deity Praj─Бpati. We agree that this is a valid identification, and it suggests a link between the two in the ancient past. We believe that Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi is a rare surviving memory of a protogonic entity linked to Indra in the RV and incorporated into Zeus in the Greek world.

Tilak - Commentary

In the hymn itself, Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi is said to have assumed the form of a yellow antelope whose head Indr─Бс╣З─л is described to have cut off. This circumstance serves to guide us in at once fixing the position of Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi in the heavens. It is the same antelope’s head that has given rise to to many myths. When the position of Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi is thus fixed, it would not be difficult to understand the various incidents described in the hymn. But without further anticipating what I have to say in the explanation of the hymn, I now proceed to examine the hymn itself. We shall then see whether the assumption which we have made regarding the character and attributes of Vr╠еish─Бkapi gives us a simple, natural, and above all, intelligible explanation of the story given in the hymn, which, as explained at present, is nothing but a bundle of disconnected, if not mutually inconsistent, statements.

There are twenty-three verses in the hymn; and of these 3, 4, 5 and 20, 21 and 22 have a direct bearing on the question we are discussing. But to understand these verses properly, it is necessary to discuss most of the other verses in the hymn, and I shall therefore examine the hymn verse by verse. I have already remarked that the hymn is one of those which have not yet been properly understood. Some of the verses have been explained by Y─Бska, but he has nowhere tried to give us the bearing of the whole story described in the hymn, S─Бyaс╣Зa’s commentary is very often simply verbal, and in many places he too is not certain about the meaning, while the Anukramaс╣З─л has been several times disregarded by S─Бyaс╣Зa himself.

On the other hand, Ludwig, Grassmann, and several other European scholars have tried in their own way to explain the legend or the story embodied in the hymn, and the latest attempt of the kind is that of Piscel and Geldner in their Vedic studies, Vol. VII., Part 1. +++(I am indebted for this information to Dr. R. G, Bh─Бс╣Зс╕Н─Бrkar, who kindly undertook to explain to me the views of German scholars on this point.)+++ These scholars hold that the hymn narrates a legend current in old days. In other words, they take it, and I think rightly, to be a historic hymn. But the question, what does the legend signify, or how did it originate, still remains unsolved. Piscel and Geldoer understand the hymn to mean that Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi went down to the south and again returned to the house of Indra. But even then the bearing of the legend is but imperfectly explained.

The occurrence of such words as d─Бsa, arya and parshu in the hymn have led some to suppose that the hymn records the story of a struggle between the Aryan and the non-Aryan races. But the hypothesis hardly explains the various incidents in the story, and the legend may therefore be said to be but still imperfectly understood. Under such circumstances any suggestion which explains the hymn better is at least entitled to a hearing. It is admitted that the hymn is a dialogue between Indra, Indr─Бс╣З─л, and Vriс╣г─Бkapi, a son of Indra as they call him.1 But there is a great divergence of opinion in assigning different verses to their deities. I shall examine these points while discussing the verses.

Piscel and Geldner introduce Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapay─л in the dialogue and distribute the verses somewhat differently thus, indra, 1, 3, 8, 12, 14, 19, 20; Indr─Бс╣З─л 2, 4, 8, 8, 9, 16, 21 ; Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi 7, 10 13 ; and Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapay─л, 11, 15, 17, 18, Verses 23 und 39 are supposed to be addressed by a stranger, the narrator.

Conclusion
Now let us see what are the leading features of the story of Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi and what they signify. We have seen that scholars differ in assigning the verses of the hymn to the different speakers, and here and there we meet with expressions and words which cannot be said to be yet satisfactorily explained. Some of the interpretations I have proposed may not again be acceptable to all. But these difficulties do not prevent us from determining the leading incidents in the legend, which may therefore be summarised somewhat as follows.

Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi is a Mr╠еga, and sacrifices are stopped where he revels. He is, however, a favourite of Indra, and consequently the latter, instead of punishing, follows him. Indr─Бс╣З─л, who has herself been offended by the Kapi, now reproaches Indra for his over fondness for the animal and threatens to punish the beast by cutting off his head and letting loose a dog at his ear. Indra intercedes and Indr─Бс╣З─л assures him that the punishment has not been inflicted on his favorite beast, but on someone else. Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi is now going down to his house and Indra, in bidding farewell to his friend, asks him to come up again to his (Indra’s) house, so that the sacrifices may be recommenced ; and, strange to say, that when Vriс╣г─Бkapi returns, in his upward march to the house of Indra, the impertinent Mr╠еga is no longer to be seen! Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, Indra and Indr─Бс╣З─л thus finally meet in the same house, without the offensive beast, and the hymn therefore concludes with a benedictory verse.

There can be little doubt that the hymn gives a legend current in old Vedic days. But no explanation has yet been suggested, which accounts for all the incidents in the story or explains how it originated. Vriс╣г─Бkapi is a Mr╠еga, and his appearance and disappearance mark the cessation and the recommencement of the sacrifices. The Indian tradition identifies him with the sun in one form or another and comparison with Greek Erikapacos point to the same conclusion. Our Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi or Mr╠еga must again be such as is liable to be conceived in the form of a head cut off from the body, and closely followed by a dog at its ear, unless we are prepared to treat the very specific threat of Indr─Бс╣З─л as meaningless except a general threat. All these incidents are plainly and intelligibly explained by taking Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi to represent the sun at the autumnal equinox, when the Dog-star or Orion commenced the equinoctial year; and, above all, we can now well understand why Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi’s house is said to be low in the south and how his Mr╠еga disappears when he goes to the house of Indra - a point which has been a hard knot for the commentators to solve.

I, therefore, conclude that the hymn gives us not only a description of the constellation of Orion and Canis (verses 4 and 5), but clearly and expressly defines the position of the sun when he passed to the north of the equator in old times (verse 22); and joined with the legend of the r╠еbhus we have here unmistakeable and reliable internal evidence of the hymns of the R╠еgveda to ascertain the period when the traditions incorporated in these hymns were first framed and conceived. In the face of these facts it is impossible to hold that the passages in the Taittir─лya saс╣Бhit─Б and the Br─Бhmaс╣Зas do not record a real tradition about the older beginning of the year.

Geldner

Tilak - Commentary

The verse has been differently interpreted by different writers.

Y─Бska (13. 4.) interprets it as referring to the rays of the sun, which (the rays) deem themselves perfectly independent of the luminary by which they were sent out.

S─Бyaс╣Зa ascribes it to Indra who says “that sacrificers, allowed by me to sacrifice to Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, have disregarded me, but are praising the lord Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi who is delighted, as my friend, in the sacrifices, where plenty of Soma is used; (but notwithstanding] this Indra is superior to all.”

M─Бdhava Bhaс╣нс╣нa, whom S─Бyaс╣Зa mentions with respect, however, thinks otherwise. He considers that the verse is addressed by Indr─Бс╣З─л to Indra, when she perceived that the sacrificers have ceased to sacrifice on account of the oblations being spoiled by an animal representing Vriс╣г─Бkapi. He would, therefore, thus interpret the verse - Says Indr─Бс╣З─л, “In places of plenty where lord Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi revels, sacrificers have give up sacrificing and disregarded Indra. My friend Indra is superior to all.”

When the very first verse is thus interpreted in three different ways, one can easily attribute the difference to an imperfect perception of the bearing of the whole hymn. To me, M─Бdhava Bhaс╣нс╣нa alone appears to have taken into consideration the verses that follow. Thus the fifth verse of the hymn states that the things of Indr─Бс╣З─л were spoilt by Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi in the form of an animal, and consequently he was beheaded. I should, however, like to refer to verse 21, wherein Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi is told that when he appears again, sacrifices would be performed. This evidently implies that they were stopped before and were to be commenced again on the re-appearance of Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi. The first verse therefore must be interpreted to mean that тАЬthe sacrifices are stopped." The root sr╠еj with vi may mean either to abandon or to allow, but the former is its natural meaning, and when verse 21 in the same hymn can be easily explained by taking the former meaning of sr╠еj with vi, it would be straining the words if we put a different interpretation on them. I am, therefore, disposed to interpret the verse after the manner of M─Бdhava Bhaс╣нс╣нa, except the last sentence.

But why should sacrifices be stopped ? What has Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi to do with them? These are very important questions, and I am sure that had they been properly answered, there would have been no difficulty in interpreting the hymn. In verse 3 we are told that Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi spoken of in this hymn, has the form of a yellow antelope. In verse 5 Indr─Бс╣З─л is prepared to cut off his head, because he offended her, and in the preceding verse ( 4th ) a dog is said to be let loose upon him. These facts -an antelope with the head cut off, and a dog closely following him are quite sufficient for the purposes of identification. They shew that the whole story is based upon the “antelope’s head” we have previously discussed ; and had Y─Бska and S─Бyaс╣Зa known that there is a constellation called dog in the heavens by the side of Mr╠еga┼Ыiras, I feel certain that they would not have hesitated to recognize in Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, the sun as represented by the constellation of Orion. But all traces of the dog, as a constellation, having been lost in the Sanskrit literature, neither Y─Бska nor s─Бyaс╣Зa could find any clue to the true meaning of the hymn.+++(5)+++

This is not, however, the only place where Y─Бska has been obliged to invent extraordinary interpretations. Not knowing that the dog represented a star, he has proposed (Nirukta 6. 20), that Vr╠еka should be understood to mean “the moon,” while usually it means a wolf or a wild dog,+++(5)+++ and it appears to me that a similar mistake has been also committed here. Comparative Mythology and Greek Astronomy have, however, thrown further light on the subject, and we must now try to interpret the hymn accordingly. Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi must, therefore, be taken to represent the sun in Orion.

But even supposing that Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi thus represents the sun in Orion, why should the sacrifices be stopped in his account? The identification of Vriс╣г─Бkapi with Orion at once furnishes us with a solution of this question. We have already seen that the dog is said to commence the new year in Rig i. 161. 13; and since Canis and Orion are close to each other, Orion may also be said to have commenced the year. The Devay─Бna, therefore, extended in those days from the heliacal to the acronycal rising of Orion, that is, when Orion rose with the sun, it was the vernal equinox, the beginning of the Devay─Бna, and six months after, when it rose at the beginning of night, it was the autumnal equinox, the end of the Devay─Бna. Now all Deva ceremonies and sacrifices could be begun and performed only during the Devay─Бna,* +++(* Jaimini Mim Dar. vi. 8. 23, and other authorities cited in Chap. II.)+++ or, as we find it in later traditions, only in the Uttar─Бyaс╣Зa. The acronycal rising of Orion was thus a signal to stop such ceremonies, and oblations could properly be said to have been spoilt by the appearance of this constellation at the beginning of night. (If Vr╠еtra is correctly identified with the constellation of Mr╠еga┼Ыiras, we may on the same theory also explain why he is called Makhasyu in Rig. x. 73, 7. The appearance of Mr╠еga, at the beginning of night indicated the commencement of the Dakс╣гiс╣З─Бyana wben sacrifices were stopped. Vr╠еtra alias Mr╠еga might thus come to be regarded as a destroyer of the sacrifices.)

But above all the burden of the song “Indra is uttara of all,” becomes specially appropriate in this case. The word uttara does not here mean superior, but “upper” implying that Indra is in the upper or the northern portion of the universe, though the sun or Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi may go down.+++(5)+++ I would therefore translate the first verse thus:-" Where my friend Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi rejoiced in the wealth of the Aryans, they gave up sacrificing and did not respect Indra. Indra is (however) in the upper (i. e., northern) part of the universe."*

(If Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapay─л is to be at all introduced in the dialogue, we may assign this verse to her. The phrases, " my friend" and “did not respect lndra,” would be more appropriate in her mouth than in that of Indra or Indr─Бс╣З─л.)

Jamison Brereton

Tilak - Commentary

Indra is here reproached for following up Vriс╣г─Бkapi, though he has offended Indr─Бс╣З─л. Says she to Indra : “O Indra! (how is it that) you run down fast after Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi and do not go anywhere else to drink soma. Indra is, &c.”

The word par─Б in this verse seems to denote the region where Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi has gone. Par─Бvat is often said to be the place in the distant or lower portion of the sphere, and is thus contrasted with arv─Бvat (Rig. viii. 13. 10). In Rig. viii. 33. 10 Indra is said to be Vr╠ес╣г─Б in the par─Бvat and also in the arv─Бvat regions. Indra is again very often spoken of as going to distant regions to see whether Vr╠еtra is duly killed. The same fact appears to be here expressed in a different form.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬIndr─Бс╣З─л speaks: You, Indra, much annoyed, hasten towards Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi; and yet you find no otherplace to drink the Soma; Indra is above all (the world).тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

[Indr─Бс╣З─л:] тАЬBut although, o Indra, you run away, beyond the wayward course of Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi,
you do not find anywhere else for soma-drinking.тАЭ
тАУ Above all Indra!

Griffith

Thou, Indra, heedless passest by the ill Vrsakapi hath wrought;
Yet nowhere else thou findest place wherein to drink the Soma juice. Supreme is Indra over all.

Tilak - Commentary

S─Бyaс╣Зa following the Anukramaс╣Зi, understands the verse as addressed by Indr─Бс╣З─л to Indra. Ludwig and Grassmann, on the other hand, take it to be addressed by Indra to Indr─Бс╣З─л; and this construction seems better than that of S─Бyaс╣Зa. It may, however, be here, once for all, remarked that though scholars thus differ in assigning verses to different deities, yet it does not, on the whole, materially alter the legend incorporated in the hymn.

Says Indra : " What has this Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, in the form of a yellow antelope, done to thee that you are so much angry with him? Was it the rich possession (wealth) of the Aryans? Indra, &c."

The form, in which Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi is here said to have appeared, should be specially noted. Harita means yellow, and yellow animals (Haritaс╕е) are said to be yoked to the carriage of Aditya in Nighaс╣Зс╣нu ( 1, 15 ). There the word is, however, understood lo be the plural of Harit, by the commentators in conformity to Rig, i. 115. 3 and . 48. 9, where the sun is said to have seven horses yoked to his carriage. But I think that the same idea may give rise to the conception that the sun is represented by a single yellow animal, and we may take the passage in the Nighaс╣Зс╣нu as referring also to the verse under consideration. I have previously alluded to the fact that the dog at the Chinvat bridge in the Parsi traditions is described as zaritem, that is, of the same colour as the antelope in the third verse. But the question of colour cannot be taken as finally settled until we first definitely decide what animal is represented by Mr╠еga.* +++(тАв See Dr. Rajendralal’s Indo-Aryans, Vol, II., p. 303.)+++

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬWhat (favour) has this tawny deer Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi done to you that you should like a liberal (benefactor)bestow upon him wealth and nourish me; Indra is above all (the world).тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

or to the thriving goods of the strangerтАФthat you are jealous of him?тАЭ тАУ Above all Indra!

Griffith

What hath he done to injure thee, this tawny beast Vrsakapi,
With whom thou art so angry now? What is the votary’s foodful store? Supreme is Indra over all.

English translation:

тАЬThis Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi whom you, Indra, cherish as your dear (son)– may the dog which chases the boar(seize) him by the ear (and) devour him; Indra is above all (the world).тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

[Indr─Бс╣З─л:] тАЬThis dear Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi of yours that you protect, o IndraтАФ the boar-hunting dog will snap at his ear.тАЭ
тАУ Above all Indra!

Griffith

Soon may the hound who hunts the boar seize him and bite him in the car,
O Indra, that Vrsakapi whom thou protectest as a friend, Supreme is Indra over all.

рдореЗ рдорд╣реНрдпрдорд┐рдиреНрджреНрд░рд╛рдгреНрдпреИ рддрд╖реНрдЯрд╛рдирд┐ рдпрдЬрдорд╛рдиреИрдГ рдХрд▓реНрдкрд┐рддрд╛рдирд┐ рдкреНрд░рд┐рдпрд╛ рдкреНрд░рд┐рдпрд╛рдгрд┐ рд╡реНрдпрдХреНрддрд╛ рд╡реНрдпрдХреНрддрд╛рдиреНрдпрд╛рдЬреНрдпреИрд░реНрд╡рд┐рд╢реЗрд╖реЗрдгрд╛рдХреНрддрд╛рдирд┐ рд╣рд╡реАрдВрд╖рд┐ рдХрд╢реНрдЪрд┐рддреН рд╡реГрд╖рд╛рдХрдкреЗрд░реНрд╡рд┐рд╖рдпреЗ рд╡рд░реНрддрдорд╛рдирдГ рдХрдкрд┐рдГ рд╡реНрдпрджреВрджреБрд╖рддреН рджреВрд╖рдпрд╛рдорд╛рд╕ ред рддрддреЛрд╜рд╣рдореН рдЕрд╕реНрдп рддрддреНрдХрдкрд┐рд╕реНрд╡рд╛рдорд┐рдиреЛ рд╡реГрд╖рд╛рдХрдкреЗрдГ рд╢рд┐рд░реЛ рдиреБ рдХреНрд╖рд┐рдкреНрд░рдВ рд░рд╛рд╡рд┐рд╖рдВ рд▓реБрдиреАрдпрд╛рдореН ред рджреБрд╖реНрдХреГрддреЗ рджреБрд╖реНрдЯрд╕реНрдп рдХрд░реНрдордгрдГ рдХрд░реНрддреНрд░реЗ рд╡реГрд╖рд╛рдХрдкрдпреЗрд╜рд╕реНрдореИ рд╕реБрдЧрдВ рд╕реБрдЦрдВ рди рднреБрд╡рдореН рдЕрд╣рдВ рди рднрд╡реЗрдпрдореН ред рдЕрд╕реНрдореИ рд╕реБрдЦрдкреНрд░рджрд╛рддреНрд░реА рди рднрд╡рд╛рдореАрддреНрдпрд░реНрдердГ ред рдЕрд╕реНрдпрд╛ рдордо рдкрддрд┐рдГ рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░рдГ рд╡рд┐рд╢реНрд╡рд╕реНрдорд╛рддреН рдЙрддреНрддрд░рдГ рее рее рез рее

Tilak - Commentary

S─Бyaс╣Зa is literally correct, but again misses the spirit, or rather has missed it throughout the hymn. Indra was reproached in the second verse for his partiality or over-kindness to Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, but Indr─Бn─л was not satisfied with it, and if Indra failed to punish the Kapi, she took the matter in her own hand. Says she : “O Indra ! as you (thus) protect this (your) favourite Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, let the dog, eager (to chase ) a hog (var─Бha ), bite him at his ear. The Kapi spoilt my favourite things.2 I shall, therefore, cut off his head, in order that an evil-doer may not enjoy happiness. Indra is in the upper (portion) of the universe,”

Here Indr─Бс╣З─л is herself prepared to punish Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi by setting the dog at his ear, and cutting off his head. I have in a previous chapter shown how the figure of Mr╠еga’s head is to be obtained in the sky. Taking the three stars in the belt of Orion as the top of the head, the dog is close by the right ear of Mr╠еga and may properly be said to bite it. The word var─Бha also points out the place where we may expect to find the dog. In Rig. i. 61.7, var─Бha is said to be killed by Vishnu beyond a mountain, which, in all probability, is the same story as that of Indra killing Vr╠еtra. +++(* ln Rig. x. 99, 6, Indra is said to have killed Trish─лr┼Ыan, had with his aid Trita killed var─Бha. )+++ A dog chasing var─Бha is therefore no other than Canis Major following the constellation of Orion, or the " antelope’s head " representing Vr╠еtra. S─Бyaс╣Зa and Y─Бska and even European scholars are silent as to who this dog is. The verses, in fact, may be said to have remained altogether unexplained hitherto, though the words themselves are simple enough and have caused no difficulty.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬThe ape has spoiled the beloved gh─л-adorned (oblations) made to me (by worshippers); let mequickly cut off his head, let me not be the giver of happiness to one who works evil; Indra is above all (the world).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

The ape: kapi = ape; also, a shorter form of Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi

Tilak - Commentary

This verse presents no difficulty. Thus satisfied, Indraс╣З─л speaks of herself as the best of women, best in every way.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬThere is no woman more amiable than I am, not one who bears fairer sons than I; nor one moretractable not one more ardent; Indra is above all (the world).тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

[Indr─Бс╣З─л:] тАЬNo woman has a better bottom than me or gives better sex. None is better at thrusting back than me, nor raises her thighs higher.тАЭ тАУ Above all Indra!

Griffith

No Dame hath ampler charms than 1, or greater wealth of love’s delights.
None with more ardour offers all her beauty to her lord’s embrace. Supreme is Indra over all.

Geldner

Es gibt keine Frau mit sch├╢nerem Hintern und zum Beischlaf geeigneter als mich, keine, die sich mehr herandr├дngt und die Lenden besser auseinanderspreizt. H├╢her als alles ist Indra.

English translation:

тАЬ[Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi speaks]: O mother, who are easy of access, it will quickly be as (you have said); may my(father) and you, mother, be united; may it delight my (father) and your head like a bird; Indra is above all (theworld).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

[Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi speaks]: O mother, who are easy of access, it will quickly be as (you have said); may my(father) and you, mother, be united; may it delight my (father) and your head like a bird; Indra is above all (theworld).

Tilak - Commentary

Indra now tries to conciliate her. S─Бyaс╣Зa, following the Anukramaс╣Зi, supposes that the seventh verse is addressed by Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi and the eighth by Indra. The only reason I can find for such an interpretation is the occurrence of the word amba, which means “mother,” and this cannot be supposed to be used by Indra. But though we avoid one difficulty in this way, we are launched into another, for the verse speaks of Indr─Бс╣З─л being pleasing “to me; " and if Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi is the speaker “me,” cannot refer to him, as Indr─Бс╣З─л is his mother and, consequently, “me” has to be interpreted to mean “my father,” and this S─Бyaс╣Зa has done.

I prefer taking amba as an affectionale and respectful mode of address, as in modern Sanskrit, and the verse presents no difficulty. We can then take both the 7th and the 8th verse together and give them a natural interpretation. I translate thus “O auspicious lady! what you say is true …… you are pleasing to me.. But oh hero-wife, with beautiful arms, pretty figure, profuse hair, and broad hips, why should you be so angry with our Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi? Indra is in the upper (part) of the universe.”

Wilson

Tilak - Commentary

Indr─Бс╣З─л replies, “This mischievous (Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi) considers me to be avir─Б (i. e., without a brave husband or son), while I am the wife of Indra, the mother of the brave, and the friend of Maruts. Indra, &c.”

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ[Indr─Бс╣З─л speaks]: This savage beast (Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi) despises me as one who has no male (protector),and yet I am the mother of male offspring, the wife of Indra, the friend of the Maruts; Indra is above all (the world).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

[Indr─Бс╣З─л speaks]: This savage beast (Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi) despises me as one who has no male (protector),and yet I am the mother of male offspring, the wife of Indra, the friend of the Maruts; Indra is above all (the world).

Jamison Brereton

[Indr─Бс╣З─л:] тАЬThis noxious creature has designs on me, as if I lacked a man [/hero].
And I have a man [/hero]тАФwith Indra as husband and the Maruts as companions.тАЭ
тАУ Above all Indra!

Griffith

This noxious creature looks on me as one bereft of hero’s love,
Yet Heroes for my sons have I, the Maruts’ Friend and Indra’s Queen. Supreme is Indra over all.

Geldner

Dieser Boshafte hat es auf mich abgesehen, als ob ich keinen Mann h├дtte. Und ich habe doch einen Mann, bin Indra┬┤s Gattin, habe die Marut zu Freunden." - H├╢her als alles steht Indra!

Grassmann

┬╗Dieser Verderber stellt mir nach wie einer von Helden verlassenen, und doch bin ich einem Helden verm├дhlt, bin des Indra Gattin, die Genossin der Maruts. H├╢her als alles ist Indra.

English translation:

тАЬThe mother who is the instrumental tutress of the ceremony, the mother of male offspring, the wife of Indra,goes first to the united sacrifice to battle, (and) is honoured (by the praisers); Indra is above all (the world).тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

Up till now the woman has (just) been accustomed to come down to the joint offering or to the festive gathering.
Now she is magnified as the Adept of Truth, as one having a hero, with Indra as husband.
тАУ Above all Indra!

Mother of Heroes, Indra’s Queen, the rite’s ordainer is extolled. Supreme is Indra over all.

Tilak - Commentary

Pischel and Geldner suppose that the first is addressed by Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi to Indr─Бс╣З─л, and the second by Vr╠ес╣гakapay─л. S─Бyaс╣Зa understands them to be addressed by Indra. Whichsoever construction we adopt, the meaning remains the same. Indr─Бс╣З─л is here told that she is highly respected everywhere; she is the blessed of all women, and that her husband never suffers from old age. This is obviously intended to pacify her.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ(Indra speaks]: I have heard that Indr─Бс╣З─л is the most fortunate among these women, for her lordIndra, who is above all (the world), does ot die of old age like other (men).тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

I have heard of Indr─Бс╣З─л as (most) fortunate among these women. for never, even in the future, will her husband die of old age.
тАУ Above all Indra!

Griffith

So have I heard Indrani called most fortunate among these Dames,
For never shall her Consort die in future time through length of days. Supreme is Indra overall.

Geldner

Unter diesen Herrinnen, so h├╢rte ich, ist Indrani die Gl├╝ckliche, denn nicht wird auch in Zukunft ihr Gemahl an Altersschw├дche sterben." - H├╢her als alles steht Indra!

Grassmann

Die Indr─Бn─л habe ich als die selige unter diesen Weibern nennen geh├╢rt; denn nie, auch in ferner Zukunft, stirbt ihr Gatte an Altersschw├дche. H├╢her als alles ist Indra.

рдорд╛рдирд╕рддрд░рдЩреНрдЧрд┐рдгреАрдХреГрддреН - рдЯрд┐рдкреНрдкрдиреА

However, the statement by Indra: тАЬyasyedam apyaс╣Г haviс╕е priyaс╣Г deveс╣гu gacchatiтАЭ тАУ the watery haviс╣г might be linked to the carrying of the seed of the gods by Erikepaius.

Tilak - Commentary

Indra says “O Indr─Бс╣З─л! I am not delighted without my friend Vriс╣г─Бkapi, of whom these favourite watery oblations reach the gods. Indra is in the upper (part) of the universe.”

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬI am not happy, Indr─Бс╣Зi, without my friend Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi; whose acceptable oblation here, purified withwater, proceeds to the gods; Indra is above all (the world).тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

[Indra:] тАЬO Indr─Бс╣З─л, I find no pleasure without my comrade Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, whose own dear watery oblation goes here and now to the gods.тАЭ тАУ Above all Indra!

Griffith

Never, Indralni, have I joyed without my friend Vrsakapi,
Whose welcome offering here, made pure with water, goeth to the Gods. Supreme is Indra over all.

Keith

I have not joyed, O Indrani,
Without my friend Vrsakapi,
Whose oblation rich in water
Goeth dear to the gods.

рд╣реЗ рд╡реГрд╖рд╛рдХрдкрд╛рдпрд┐ ред рдХрд╛рдорд╛рдирд╛рдВ рд╡рд░реНрд╖рдХрддреНрд╡рд╛рджрднреАрд╖реНрдЯрджреЗрд╢рдЧрдордирд╛рдЪреНрдЪреЗрдиреНрджреНрд░реЛ рд╡реГрд╖рд╛рдХрдкрд┐рдГ ред рддрд╕реНрдп рдкрддреНрдирд┐ ред рдпрджреНрд╡рд╛ ред рд╡реГрд╖рд╛рдХрдкреЗрд░реНрдордо рдорд╛рддрд░рд┐рддреНрдпрд░реНрдердГ ред рд░реЗрд╡рддрд┐ рдзрдирд╡рддрд┐ рд╕реБрдкреБрддреНрд░реЗ рд╢реЛрднрдирдкреБрддреНрд░реЗ рд╕реБрд╕реНрдиреБрд╖реЗ рд╢реЛрднрдирд╕реНрдиреБрд╖реЗ рд╣реЗ рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░рд╛рдгрд┐ рддреЗ рддрд╡рд╛рдпрдореН рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░рдГ рдЙрдХреНрд╖рдгрдГ рд╕реЗрдЪрдирд╕рдорд░реНрдерд╛рдиреН рдЖрджреБ рдЕрдирдиреНрддрд░рдореЗрд╡ ред рд╢реАрдШреНрд░рдореЗрд╡реЗрддреНрдпрд░реНрдердГ ред рдкрд╢реВрдиреН рдШрд╕рддреН рдкреНрд░рд╛рд╢реНрдирд╛рддреБ ред рдХрд┐рдВрдЪ рдХрд╛рдЪрд┐рддреНрдХрд░рдореН ред рдХрдВ рд╕реБрдЦрдореН ред рддрд╕реНрдпрд╛рдЪрд┐рддреН рд╕рдВрдШрдГ ред рддрд╕реНрдХрд░рдВ рд╣рд╡рд┐рдГ рдкреНрд░рд┐рдпрдореН рдЗрд╖реНрдЯрдВ рдХреБрд░реНрд╡рд┐рддрд┐ рд╢реЗрд╖рдГ ред рдХрд┐рдВрдЪ рддреЗ рдкрддрд┐рдГ рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░рдГ рд╡рд┐рд╢реНрд╡рд╕реНрдорд╛рддреН рдЙрддрд░рдГ ред рддрдерд╛ рдЪ рдпрд╛рд╕реНрдХрдГ - тАШ рд╡реГрд╖рд╛рдХрдкрд╛рдпрд┐ рд░реЗрд╡рддрд┐ рд╕реБрдкреБрддреНрд░реЗ рдордзреНрдпрдореЗрди рд╕реБрд╕реНрдиреБрд╖реЗ рдорд╛рдзреНрдпрдорд┐рдХрдпрд╛ рд╡рд╛рдЪрд╛ ред рд╕реНрдиреБрд╖рд╛ рд╕рд╛рдзреБрд╕рд╛рджрд┐рдиреАрддрд┐ рд╡рд╛ рд╕рд╛рдзреБрд╕рд╛рдирд┐рдиреАрддрд┐ рд╡рд╛ ред рдкреНрд░рд┐рдпрдВ рдХреБрд░реБрд╖реНрд╡ рд╕реБрдЦрд╛рдЪрдпрдХрд░рдВ рд╣рд╡рд┐рдГ рд╕рд░реНрд╡рд╕реНрдорд╛рджреНрдп рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░ рдЙрддреНрддрд░рдГ’ (рдирд┐рд░реБ. резреи, реп) рдЗрддрд┐ рее

Tilak - Commentary

This seems to be also addressed by Indra to Indr─Бс╣З─л, who is here called Vr╠ес╣г─Бkap─Бy─л. This latter name has caused a difference of opinion, some considering Vr╠ес╣гakap─Бy─л to be the mother, some the wife of Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi. +++(See Max Muller’s Lectures on the Science of Language, Vol. II., 538)+++ I do not see how the wife of Vriс╣г─Бkapi as such, could be introduced in the song, unless Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi is understood to be the name of Indra himself. Commentators, who take Vr╠ес╣г─Бkap─Бy─л to mean the wife of Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, accordingly adopt the latter view. Pischel and Geldner think that the verse is addressed by Vriс╣г─Бkapi to his wife Vr╠ес╣г─Бkap─Бy─л.

The verse means, “O rich Vr╠ес╣г─Бkap─Бy─л having a good son and a daughter-in-law, let Indra swallow the bulls, your favourite and delightful oblation, Indra &c.” There has been much speculation as to who could be the son and the daughter-in-law of Vr╠ес╣г─Бkap─Бy─л. But if Vr╠ес╣г─Бkap─Бy─л be understood to mean the wife of Indra, it causes no such difficulty. The adjectives “having a good son.” &c, are simply complimentary, corresponding to the statement of Indr─Бс╣З─л, that she was the “mother of the brave” in verse 9. Indra accepting her statements, asks her to allow him to swallow the watery oblations said to come from Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi in the last verse. The words priyam and havis are the same in both the verses; and I think that both of them refer to the same oblations.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ[Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi speaks]: O mother of Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi, wealthy, possessing excellent sons, possessingexcellent daughters-in-law, let Indra eat your bulls, (give him) the beloved and most delightful gh─л, Indra is aboveall (the world).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Mother of Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi: Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapayin = wife of Indra; Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi may be a name of Indra, as theshowerer of benefits

Jamison Brereton

[Indra:] тАЬO wife of Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi [=Indr─Бс╣З─л], rich, having good sons and daughters-in-law,
Indra will eat your oxen and the dear oblation that brings about whatever (you want).тАЭ
тАУ Above all Indra!

Griffith

Wealthy Vrsakapayi, blest with sons and consorts of thy sons,
Indra will eat thy bulls, thy dear oblation that effecteth much. Supreme is Indra over all.

Geldner

Frau Vrisakapi, reiche, Mutter sch├╢ner S├╢hne und sch├╢ner Schwiegert├╢chter! Indra soll deine Stiere genie├Яen und die erw├╝nschte einigerma├Яen gen├╝gende Opferspende." - H├╢her als alles steht Indra!

Grassmann

O reiche Vrisch─Бkap─Бj─л, die du treffliche S├╢hne und Schwiegert├╢chter hast, Indra wird deine Stiere verzehren und den lieben, sehr wirksamen Opfertrank. H├╢her als alles ist Indra.

Tilak - Commentary

Indra, satisfied with the prospect of getting the oblations, describes his appetite: “Twenty and ffteen oxen are being cooked for me; I shall eat them and be fat. Both the sides of my belly will be filled up. Indra, &c.”

The practice of sacrificing bulls to Indra seems to have been out of date even at the time of the R╠еgveda (cf. i, 164. 43, where it is said to be an old custom). But the old custom could not be entirely forgotten, and if real bulls were not offered to Indra, poets supposed that clouds or stars might answer the same purpose. The number 35 mentioned in the verse may thus refer to the Nakс╣гatras (28), and planets (7). But this explanation is doubtful and I cannot suggest a better one.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ[Indra speaks]: The worshippers dress for me fifteen (and) twenty bulls; I eat them and (become) fat,they fill both sides of my belly; Indra is above all (the world).тАЭ

Like as a bull with pointed horn, loud bellowing amid the herds,
Sweet to thine heart, O Indra, is the brew which she who tends thee pours. Supreme is Indra over all.

Tilak - Commentary

The fifteenth and the sixteenth seem to be addressed to Indra by Indr─Бс╣З─л, and the seventeenth to Indr─Бс╣З─л by Indra. In the fifteenth Indr─Бni, according to S─Бy─Бс╣Зa, asked Indra to sport with her just as a bull, with pointed horns, roars amongst a number of cows. The next two verses do not appear to be relevant to our purpose. We may therefore pass these over, and resume the thread of the story. Pischel and Geldner suppose that the 17th and 18th verses are addressed by Vr╠ес╣гakap─Бy─л.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ[Indra speaks]: He who is endowed with vigour begets not progeny, but he who is impotent; Indra isabove all (the world).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

[Indra speaks]: He who is endowed with vigour begets not progeny, but he who is impotent; Indra isabove all (the world).

Jamison Brereton

[Indr─Бс╣З─л:] тАЬHe is not master, for whose (penis) the hairy (vulva) gapes when it [=erect penis] has sat down.
He is master, whose penis hangs (limp) between his thighs.тАЭ
тАУ Above all Indra!

English translation:

тАЬ[Indr─Бс╣З─л speaks]: Let this Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi, Indra, take a dead wild ass, (let him take) a knife (to cut it up),a fire-plural ce (to cook it), a new saucepan, and a cart full of fuel; Indra is above all (the world).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

A dead wild ass:parasvantam = parasvam, i.e. one who is of his own nature, i.e. an ape, kapi; a fire plural ce: s┼лn─Б; cf. Manu3.68

Tilak - Commentary

Indr─Бс╣З─л is now conciliated, and says that she has not killed Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, but some one else. The verse thus means, “O Indra ! Let Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi get the slain animal-an animal which was quite different from Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi’s. Let him at once have a knife, a fire-place, a new vessel, and a cart-load of firewood (to cook the killed animal). ladra, &c.тАЭ Thus by the intercession of Indra, Indr─Бс╣З─л was moved, and at last undid or rather explained away her previous act of decapitation.

Pischel and Geldaer translate the verse very nearly as I have done. They, however, consider it to be addressed by Vr╠еsh─Бkap─Бy─л and translate parasvantam by “wild.” This does not explain what dead animal is here referred to. It is, I think, more natural to suppose that the dead animal here spoken of is the same as that described in Verse 6, and one whose head Indr─Бс╣З─л is there said to be ready to cut off.

Indr─Бс╣З─л now says that this dead animal should be given to Vr╠ес╣гh─Бkapi, especially as Indra has already got his oblations of bulls. I have already shown that there were several legends about the " antelope’s head.” It seems that Indr─Бс╣З─л, referring to some of them, assures Indra that it was not Vriс╣г─Бkapi in the form of the antelope which she killed, but some one else (literally parasvantam - representing another than Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, as S─Бyaс╣Зa takes it.)

Thereon Indra, having thus saved Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi by his intercession, observes, “Thus do I go seeing and discriminating between a dasa and an arya ; I take my drink from those that prepare Soma juice and cook the oblations, and thus behold or protect the intelligent sacrificers.” In another word, Indra is glad that he has saved an Arya, and triumphantly declares that he is always careful to distinguish between an Arya and a D─Бsa, the latter of whom he would punish and kill, e.g., Vr╠еtra, who is said to be a D─Бsa, Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi being thus saved Indra, in the following verses, bids him a farewell, wishing for a safe journey and speedy return. These verses are very important for our present purpose, and I shall therefore examine them singly.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ[Indra speaks]: Here I come to the (sacrifice) looking upon (the worshippers), distinguishing the D─Бsaand the ─Аrya; I drink (the Soma) of the (worshipper), who effuses (the Soma) with mature (mind); I look upon theintelligent (sacrificer); Indra is above all (the world).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Cf. Muir, Sanskrit Texts, vol. 2, p. 374

Jamison Brereton

[Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi:] тАЬHere I go earnestly looking, distinguishing between D─Бsa and ─Аrya.
(While) I drink (the offering) of the guileless presser, I kept looking for the wise.тАЭ
тАУ Above all Indra!

Griffith

Distinguishing the Dasa and the Arya, viewing all, I go.
I look upon the wise, and drink the simple votary’s Soma juice. Supreme is Indra over all.

Geldner

Ich ziehe umher, Umschau haltend und zwischen Dasa und Arier scheidend. Nun trinke ich bei dem, der einen schlichten Trank braut. Nach einem Sachkundigen habe ich ausgeschaut. - H├╢her als alles steht Indra!

Grassmann

Ich hier gehe scharf blickend einher, Arier und Barbaren unterscheidend; ich trinke den Soma dessen, der mit redlichem Sinne ihn braut, und beschaue [mit Wohlgefallen] den verst├дndigen. H├╢her als alles ist Indra.

Tilak - Commentary

In this verse Indra asks Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi to go to his house (astam) and then return afterwards to the house (gr╠еhas) of Indra. But the question is where is Vpipb─Бkapi’s house and where is that of Indra ? The words in the original are dhanva, kr╠еntatra and ned─лyas. Vriс╣г─Бkapi is asked to go to dhanva, which is also kr╠еntatra. S─Бyaс╣Зa taka dhanva to mean a desert and kr╠еntatra in the sense that “the trees therein are cut off.” But this meaning does not quite suit the context. What is meant by saying that Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, who is admittedly the sun in a different form, should go to a forest? Where is that forest, and what does it imply?

Dhanva is a word that occurs several times in the R╠еgveda. In Rig. i. 38. 8 it is said to consist of three yojanas and is contrasted with the earth. S─Бyaс╣Зa there understands it to mean “sky or heavens,” and I see no reason why we should not interpret the word in the same way in this verse. Dhanva therefore means “sky” or “heavens.” But is it the vault above with three stages? No, the poet qualifies the idea by kr╠еntatra, meaning “cut off.” It is thus evidently the portion of the heavens which is cut off. In other words, the idea here denoted is the same as that expressed by the phrase avarodhanam divah where heavens are closed, " where the view is obstructed," in Rig. ix. 113. 8. Dhanva, which is kr╠еtantra, * thus denotes the innermost part of the celestial sphere, the southern hemisphere or the Pitr╠еy─Бna. (* The only other place where kr╠еntatra is used in the R╠еgveda is v. 27. 13, which Y─Бska and S─Бyaс╣Зa both interpret to mean that тАЬwaters come up from kr╠еntatra" 1. 8., a cloud." But it may be a well asked it Kr╠еntatr─Бt cannot here mean " from below." )

The poet knows that the vault of the heavens above him has three halts or stages which Vishс╣Зu is said to have used as his three steps (Rig. i. 22. 17). But of the nether world the poet has no definite knowledge, and he therefore cannot specify the yojanas or the stages it contains. Thus he simply says that there are some yojanas therein. The first part of the verse may now be translated thus: “O Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi! go to the house (in) the celestial sphere which is cut off and which contains some yojanas or stages.” In short, Indra means that Vpiс╣гakapi should now descend into the southern hemispere.

The latter part of the verse literally means “and come to our house from ned─лyas.” Now ned─лyas is again a word which neither Y─Бska dor S─Бyaс╣Зa seem to have properly understood. P─Бс╣Зini (v. 3.63) tells us that ned─лyas is the comparative of antika. Now ned─лyas cannot possibly be derived from antika by any change in the form of the latter word. P─Бnini therefore considers neda to be a substitute for antika, when the comparative form is to be derived. This is equivalent to saying that ‘bet’ is to be substituted for ‘good’ in deriving the comparative form of ‘good’ in English. I need not say how far such an expla nation would be regarded satisfactory. My own view is that ned─лyas had lost its positive form in the times of P─Бс╣Зini, or perhaps its positive form was never in use like that of superior in English. But P─Бс╣Зini, who, as a grammarian, felt bound to account for all the forms, connected ned─лyas with antika, probably because the ordinary meaning of ned─лyas in his time was the same as that of the comparative form of antika. But we cannot infer from this that ned─лyas might not have meant anything else in the days of P─Бс╣Зini. Paс╣Зini might liave taken into account only the most ordinary sense of the word, and finding that a positive form was wanting connected it with the word which expressed the ordinary meaning in the positive form. The fact that P─Бс╣Зini considers ned─лyas as the comparative of antika does not therefore preclude us from assuming, if we have other grounds to do so, that ned─лyas originally meant something else in addition to its present sense ; for P─Бс╣Зini speaks of the form and not of the meaning of ned─лyas. Having thus shown that the authority of Par╠еni is not against me, I shall now give my meaning of ned─лyas. I think it means lower, being akin to neath, beneath, nether and corresponding words in other languages. (тАв Bopp derives O. H. G. nidar from sk. ni down, and disapproves Grimm’s suggestion that it should be traced to a Gothic verb niliun, natit, nethum, and divided as nid-ar, ar being a omparative termination, (Bopp. Com. Gr. Eng. Te, 1860, Vol. I., 382). K. Brugermann compares Sk. ned─лyas with Av. nazd-yar meaning nearer, and derives the words from nazd (ni down and zd to sit). Cf. Sk. n─лda Lat. n─лdu, O. Ir. net, O H.G. nest= a nesting place (Comp. Gr. i., ┬з 591, ii, $84, 135). Both bopp and Brugmann do not propose any new meaning of ned─лyas. But it is evident that whichsoever derivation we adopt the word is connected with ni down, and if we find passages in the Brahmanas where it is contrasted with upariс╣гс╣нh─Бt, we can, I think, safely understand ned─лyas to mean ’lower’ as suggested by its etymology ; ’nearer’ is secondary meaning.)

The suggestion, I know, will be received by some with surprise and suspicion, and I must give my grounds for proposing a new meaning. There is no passage in the R╠еgveda where the use of ned─лyas might be cousidered as definitely deciding its meaning. In Rig. v. 52. 6, viii. 26. 10 and x. 101. 3, nediс╣гtha or ned─лyas might be supposed to mean lowest or lower. But the passages are not conclusive on this point, as the word there used might also be understood to mean ’nearest,’ nearer,’ according to Paс╣Зini. In the Br─Бhmaс╣Зas we, however, meet with more decisive passages. Thus in the Aitareya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa vi. 27 ned─лyas is contrasted with upariс╣гс╣нh─Бt.* +++(* рдЙрдкрд░рд┐рд╖реНрдард╛рдиреН рдиреЗрджреАрдпрд╕рд┐ рднрд╛рдЧреЗ Ait. Br. vi. 27.)+++ B├╢htlingk and Roth give a passage from the Kaс╣нhaka recension of Yajurveda (28. 4), which says, the ascends (─Бrohat) to the heavens from the nethiс╣гс╣нha world."+ +++(рдиреЗрджрд┐рд╖реНрдард╛рджреЗрд╡ рд╕реНрд╡рд░реНрдЧрд▓реЛрдХрдорд╛рд░реЛрд╣рддрд┐ред Kathak. 28. 4)+++ Here the word ‘ascend’ clearly shows that the netishtha world 10981 be undeistood to mean the lowest world,’ world at the bottom. In the Tandya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa iii. 4, 2, (3, 2) there occurs a passage where the directions for lowering the tone are given as follows:-“Just as after creeping up to the top of a high tree (a man) gradually comes lower and lower so, &c.”. +++(* рдпрдерд╛ рдорд╣рд╛рд╡реГрдХреНрд╖рд╕реНрдпрд╛рдЧреНрд░рдВ рд╕реГрдкреНрддреНрд╡рд╛ рдиреЗрджреАрдпрдГ рд╕рдЩреНрдХреНрд░рдорд╛рддреН рд╕рдЩреНрдХреНрд░рд╛рдорддреНрдпреН, рдПрд╡рдореН рдПрддрдиреН рдиреЗрджреАрдпрдГ рд╕рдЩреНрдХреНрд░рдордпрд╛ рдиреЗрджреАрдпрдГ рд╕рдЩреНрдХреНрд░рд╛рдорд╛рддреН рд╕рдЩреНрдХреНрд░рд╛рдорддрд┐ред Tan. Br. iii. 4.2. )+++ The word for lowering in the text in ned─лyas sankram─Бt, and there is no possibility of mistaking its meaning. In the T─Бndya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa ii. 1. 3 the raising of the tone is described as ascending from top to top (agr─Бt agram); and ned─лyas sankrama must, therefore, mean a gradual lowering of the voice. In fact, ned─лyas sankrama represents the same idea as low-er-ing, that is, not taking a sudden leap down but descending from the highest point to the next lower, and so on.

In all these places S─Бyaс╣Зa explains ned─лyas as meaning ’nearer’ according to P─Бс╣Зini; but in every case he has to strain the words to suit the context. It was not, however, S─Бyaс╣Зa’s fault ; for after ned─лyas was once assigned to antika, all traces of its old meaning were naturally lost, and none dared to question Paс╣Зini’s authority. But we now know that in other languages neath means low, and in several passages in the Br─Бhmaс╣Зas, we find ned─лyas contrasted with ‘upper’ or ’top.’ This, in my opinion, is sufficient to prove that ned─лyas meant lower in the Vedic times. I have already shown that the authority of P─Бс╣Зini is not against understanding the word in this way. All that he has laid down is that ned─лyas having no positive form should be derived from antika without saying whether ned─лyas was or was not used in any other sense. I am therefore inclined to think that ned─лyas might have had more than one meaning even in P─Бс╣Зini’s time, but he took the most ordinary meaning and derived the comparative form from antika. This is course of time served in its turn to restrict the denotation of the word only to one meaning, viz., ’nearer.’

I would therefore translate the verse thus, “O Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi! go to the house the celestial sphere which is cut off and which contains some (unknown) yojanas or stages. From your nether house come to our house. Indra is in the upper (portion) of the universe.” Nediyas is thus contrasted with uttara in the burden of the song. Both are comparative forms. Indra is in the uttara (upper) regions, while Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi is going to the ned─лyas (lower) world ; and Indra expects or rather requests Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi to come back again to his(Indra’s) house. That is the gist of the whole verse.

The idea that the sun falls down from the autumnal equinox is an old one. To Ait. Br. iii. 18 and in Taitt. Br. i. 3. 12. 1 the ceremonies on on the Viс╣г┼лv─Бn or the equinoctial day in a satra are described, and there we are told that “gods were afraid of the sun falling down from the sky and so supported him,” and being thus supported he “became uttara to all”. The ait. Br. iii. 18 has thus the same word uttara that we have in this verse, and it is natural to suppose that both relate to the same subject. I have also quoted a passage from the Aitareya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa where ned─лyas is contrasted with upariс╣гhс╣нat. From these I infer that the verse, we are now considering, describes the descent of the sun into the southern or the lower hemisphere, and that Indra asks him to come back again to the house of Gods, i.e., the northern hemisphere.

I have already given in full my reasons for understanding ned─лyas in a different sense. But I may remark that, even accepting the common meaning of the word, the verse may still be interpreted in the way I have suggested.

Wilson

Tilak - Commentary

Vr╠ес╣гikapi has gone down to the netherworld. This verse now describes what Indra will do when he returns. Says Indra, “O Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi ! You, the destroyer of sleep, who are going to the house, come back again, again by (your) way. We would perform the sacrifices. Indra, &c.” The verse thus distinctly refers to the recommencement of the sacrifices in the Devay─Бna or the Uttar─Бyaс╣Зa as understood in old days. The word suvita is from the same root as vait─Бnika and kalpy─Бvahai is from kl╠еp, the root which gives us the word kalpa in kalpas┼лtras. Savita kalpy─Бvahai thus means тАЬwe would perform the vait─Бnika ceremonies," which, as described in the first verse, were stopped when the sun went down to the neither world I may also here point out that the house in the nether world or, as S─Бyaс╣Зa interprets it, the house of the enemy is called asta - literally ’thrown’, while Indra’s house is called gr╠еha. The sun goes down to the asta and returns up to the gr╠еha of Indra. This verse, in so far as it speaks of the recommencement of sacrificial ceremonies, confirms the interpretation I have proposed for the preceding verse.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬCome back, Vс╣Ыс╣г─Бkapi, that we may do what is agreeable to you; you, who are the destroyer ofsleep, come home again by the road; Indra is above all (the world).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Destroyer of sleep: i.e., the sun; cf. Nirukta12.28

Jamison Brereton

[Indr─Бс╣З─л:] тАЬCome here again, Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi. We two will arrange easy passages (for you),
who, as sleep-destroyer [/-attainer], go home again (to death) along the path.тАЭ
тАУ Above all Indra!

Griffith

Turn thee again Vrsakapi: we twain will bring thee happiness.
Thou goest homeward on thy way along this path which leads to sleep. Supreme is Indra over all.

рдЧрддреНрд╡рд╛ рдкреБрдирд░рд╛рдЧрддрдВ рд╡реГрд╖рд╛рдХрдкрд┐рдорд┐рдиреНрджреНрд░рдГ рдкреГрдЪреНрдЫрддрд┐ ред рд╣реЗ рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░ рдкрд░рдореИрд╢реНрд╡рд░реНрдпрд╡рдиреН рд╣реЗ рд╡реГрд╖рд╛рдХрдкреЗ рдпреВрдпрдореН рдЙрджрдЮреНрдЪрдГ рдЙрджреНрдЧрд╛рдорд┐рдирдГ рд╕рдиреНрддреЛ рдорджреНрдЧреГрд╣рдореН рдЕрдЬрдЧрдиреНрддрди рдЖрдЧрдЪреНрдЫ ред рдПрдХрд╕реНрдпрд╛рдкрд┐ рдмрд╣реБрд╡рдЪрдирдВ рдкреВрдЬрд╛рд░реНрдердореН ред рддрддреНрд░ рднрд╡рддрдГ рд╕рдВрдмрдиреНрдзреА рдкреБрд▓реНрд╡рдШрдГ рдмрд╣реВрдирд╛рдВ рднреМрдорд░рд╕рд╛рдирд╛рдорддреНрддрд╛ рд╕реНрдпрдГ рд╕рдГ рдореГрдЧрдГ рдХреНрд╡ рдЕрднреВрддреН рдЬрдирдпреЛрдкрдирдГ рдЬрдирд╛рдирд╛рдВ рдореЛрджрдпрд┐рддрд╛ рдореГрдЧрдГ рдХрдВ рд╡рд╛ рджреЗрд╢рдореН рдЕрдЧрдиреН рдЕрдЧрдЪреНрдЫрддреН ред рд╕реЛрд╜рд╣рдореН рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░рдГ рд╡рд┐рд╢реНрд╡рд╕реНрдорд╛рддреН рдЙрддреНрддрд░рдГ ред рдпрджреНрд╡рд╛ рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░рд╛рдгреАрд╡рд╛рдХреНрдпрдорд┐рджрдореН ред рдЕрддреНрд░ рдпрд╛рд╕реНрдХрдГ—- тАШ рдпрджреБрджреБрдЮреНрдЪреЛ рд╡реГрд╖рд╛рдХрдкреЗ рдЧреГрд╣рдорд┐рдиреНрджреНрд░рд╛рдЬрдЧрдордд рдХреНрд╡ рд╕реНрдп рдкреБрд▓реНрд╡рдШреЛ рдореГрдЧрдГ рдХреНрд╡ рд╕ рдмрд╣реНрд╡рд╛рджреА рдореГрдЧрдГ ред рдореГрдЧреЛ рдорд╛рд░реНрд╖реНрдЯреЗрд░реНрдЧрддрд┐рдХрд░реНрдордгрдГ ред рдХрдордЧрдорджреНрджреЗрд╢рдВ рдЬрдирдпреЛрдкрдирдГ ’ (рдирд┐рд░реБ. резрей. рей ) рдЗрддрд┐ рее

Tilak - Commentary

This is the most important verse in the whole hymn. It describes the circumstances under which Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi will return to Indra’s house. Literally rendered it means, “O mighty Vriс╣г─Бkapi! 3 When you rising upwards (or rather northwards) would come to (our) house, where would that great sinner Mr╠еga be? Where he, who misleads people, would go? Indra, &c.”

Now Y─Бska, in whose days all traces of Canis being once a star in the heavens were lost, could not understand what to make of the statement “where would that great singer Mr╠еga be?” It means that Mr╠еga would not be seen, would not be visible, when Vriс╣г─Бkapi goes to the house of Indra; but Y─Бska did not perceive what was intended by such a statement. He could not conceive that the constellation of Mr╠еga┼Ыiras would be invisible, when the sun in his upward march would be there at the beginning of the Devay─Бna, that is, when he comes to the house of Indra, and therefore he proposed to interpret Mr╠еga in the sense of тАЬthe sun" (Nirukta !3, 3). Mr╠еga, says he, is derived from mij to go, and means “going,” “one who goes and goes and never stops,’ in other words, “the sun.” Now, says his commentator, when a person goes into a house he cannot be seen by the outsiders. So Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, when he goes to the house, cannot be seen by the people on the earth!

I do not think that I need point out the highly artificial and inconsistent character of this explanation. The word Mr╠еga, so far as I know, is no where used in the R╠еgveda in this sense. Again, if the word Mr╠еga in the third verse of this hymn is to be understood as meaning an antelope, is it not natural enough to suppose that the same Mr╠еga is re ferred to in this verse? Then, again, how can the sun be said to become invisible to the people when he is in the house of Indra? Nor can he be invisible to Indra whose house he enters. What can, in such a case, be the propriety of the word udancha or тАЬ rising upwards”? If Mr╠еga means the sun according to Y─Бska, we shall have to suppose that the rising sun was invisible, a clear contradiction in terms. I am sure Y─Бska, here, tried to explain away the difficulty in the same way as he has done in the case of Vr╠еka. But, in the present instance, the solution he has proposed is, on the face of it, highly inconsistent, so much so that even S─Бyaс╣Зa, does not follow it.

S─Бyaс╣Зa, however, has nothiag else to propose, and he quietly leaves the word Mr╠еga as it is and unexplained in his commentary. In short, both S─Бyaс╣Зa and Y─Бska have found the verse too difficult to explain. The meaning I have proposed explains the verse in a natural and a simple manner, and further corroborates the statement in the R╠еgveda previously referred to vis., “Canis awakened the r╠еbhus at the end of the year. "

In the Taittir─лya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa i. 8. 2. 1, we are told that the Vedic priests, e.g., Maisoni, observed the position of the sun amongst stars in the morning, and, as the Nakс╣гatras disappear when the sun rises, they determined the position by observing what Nakс╣гatra rose a little before the sun. The present verse records an observation to make which no greater skill is required. It tells us that when Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi went to the house of Indra his Mr╠еga was not visible anywhere, thus clearly indicating that sun rose with Orion on that day. The word udancha is especially remarkable in this case. The sun must be udancha when he goes to the house of Indra, which, the burden of the song tells us, is in the northern or the upper part of the universe. This verse, therefore, clearly describes not merely the rising sun, but the position of the rising sun amongst the constellations when he is at the vernal equinox, the entrance of the house of gods or the house of Indra. S─Бyaс╣Зa and Y─Бska have completely missed this point and have made Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi represent the rising sun as an unnecessary rival to Savita (Nirukta 12. 12).

If the meaning I have proposed is correct, we have here a record of the position of the sun at the vernal equinox. I take pulvaghas in the original to mean “great sinner;” but it may be translated as Y─Бska proposes by “omnivorous” or “voracious.” But in either case I would take it as referring to the antelope’s spoiling the things of Indr─Бс╣З─л. The point is that the sinning Mr╠еga would not be with Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi when he again goes to the house of Indra, and Indr─Бс╣З─л would have no cause to complain of the presence of the odious Mr╠еga at the time.

Wilson

рдорд╛рдирд╕рддрд░рдЩреНрдЧрд┐рдгреАрдХреГрддреН - рдЯрд┐рдкреНрдкрдиреА

We also suspect that in the last с╣Ыk of the s┼лkta, there is an allusion to the human genesis via ManuтАЩs daughter Par┼Ыu (mother of the Iranians?) that gives a protogonic link.

Tilak - Commentary

S─Бyaс╣Зa translates, “O arrow I Manu’s daughter, named Par┼Ыu, gave birth to twenty (sons) together. Let her whose belly was big be happy! Indra is in the upper (portion) of the universe.” I cannot, however, understand what it means. Parshu, according to S─Бyaс╣Зa, is a Mr╠еg─л or a female antelope. But why address the arrow to give happiness to her ? Can it have any reference to the arrow with which Orion was killed ? Then who are these twenty sons ? Are they the same as twenty mentioned in verse 14 ? Is it likely that twenty alone are mentioned leaving the additional fifteen to be understood from the context?

The concluding verse undoubtedly appears to be benedictory. But I have not found a satisfactory solution of the above questions, Perhaps bhala meaning ‘auspicious,’ may be used for Vr╠ес╣г─Бkapi, and Indra addressing him pronounces benediction on the female that gave birth to the yellow antelope and several other stars that are supposed to be either killed or swallowed by Indra in this hymn. But I cannot speak with certainty on the point and must leave the verse as it is.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬThe daughter of Manu, Par┼Ыu by name, bore twenty children at once; may good fortune, O arrow ofIndra, befall her whose belly was so prolific; Indra is above all (the world).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Indra is the deity invoked: Nirukta13.3

Jamison Brereton

ManuтАЩs daughter, Par┼Ыu (тАЬRibтАЭ) by name, gave birth to twenty at once. (Good) fortune indeed was there for her whose belly (labor-pain) vexed. тАУ Above all Indra!

Griffith

Daughter of Manu, Parsu bare a score of children at a birth.
Her portion verily was bliss although her burthen caused her grief.

Geldner

Parsu mit Namen, die Tochter des Manu, gebar zwanzig Kinder auf einmal. Wahrlich, Gl├╝ck hat sie gehabt, deren Leib die Wehen bekam. - H├╢her als alles steht Indra!

Grassmann

Das menschliche Weib, Par├зu mit Namen, hat zwanzig Kinder auf einmal geboren; Heilung ward ihr zu Theil, deren Leib krank war. H├╢her als alles ist Indra.
